    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

  <span id="freeText15534555005946103380" style="display:none">A cursed book. A missing professor. Some nefarious men in gray suits. And a dreamworld called the Troposphere?<br /> <br />Ariel Manto has a fascination with nineteenth-century scientists--especially Thomas Lumas and <i>The End of Mr. Y</i>, a book no one alive has read. When she mysteriously uncovers a copy at a used bookstore, Ariel is launched into an adventure of science and faith, consciousness and death, space and time, and everything in between.<br /> <br />Seeking answers, Ariel follows in Mr. Y’s footsteps: She swallows a tincture, stares into a black dot, and is transported into the Troposphere--a wonderland where she can travel through time and space using the thoughts of others. There she begins to understand all the mysteries surrounding the book, herself, and the universe. Or is it all just a hallucination?</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/469407-the-plot-has-a-good-flow-but-i-just-can-t">The plot has a good flow, but I just can&#39;t get over the pretentiousness. How does every conversation somehow turn into a philosophical discussion? Every single one. It intrigued me at the start, but it grew tiresome to read through, and, I just ended up skimming.</a>
</div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24725879?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24725879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14833496645289107667">Oh my, I think I am cursed. Today at least I am under a spell. The day starts normally: waking up, checking the alarmclock, seeing the stack of books next to my bed. One book especially grabs my attention. I decide to read one more chapter, as I still have plenty of time before work.<br/><br/>I read and read, one chapter, two chapter, three chapters...<br/>- I can start later, I will work longer -<br/>...Words, letters, paragraphs...<br/>- I am ill, I need to stay in bed, I will work over the weekend -<br/>...Plots, subplots</span>
  <span id="freeText14833496645289107667" style="display:none">Oh my, I think I am cursed. Today at least I am under a spell. The day starts normally: waking up, checking the alarmclock, seeing the stack of books next to my bed. One book especially grabs my attention. I decide to read one more chapter, as I still have plenty of time before work.<br /><br />I read and read, one chapter, two chapter, three chapters...<br />- I can start later, I will work longer -<br />...Words, letters, paragraphs...<br />- I am ill, I need to stay in bed, I will work over the weekend -<br />...Plots, subplots, twists...<br />- I might develop a fever, I have a fever-<br />...I must read, must read, must read, read, read...<br /><br />So I stay in bed the whole day, only getting up to get a bowl of cereal and later a bag of raisins. Lying in bed, at four in the afternoon I fall asleep again. It is hard to separate dream from reality. When the phone rings I expect the main character to be calling.<br /><br />At six I finish the book, and instantly regret it. I am so bad at postponing gratification. I just have to eat the whole chocolate bar at once instead of just one square at a time.<br /><br />So I envy you.<br />You can still read this cursed book square by square.<br />But it will have you aching for more.<br /><br />See you in the Troposphere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14833496645289107667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2263186170?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/63168028-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2263186170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6760505447727455528"><b>Transferance</b><br/>Several years ago I was passing a charity shop and through the window, I spotted a number of books for sale, so I ventured inside. This book really stood out so I bought it. I was absolutely intrigued when I realised that the main protagonist (Ariel Manto) bought her copy of <i>The End of Mr Y</i> in a second-hand bookshop. I opened the cover and started reading hoping that my expectation of a fantasy adventure wasn’t just about to be smashed. <br/><br/>From the first page, I was hooked, an immediate</span>
  <span id="freeText6760505447727455528" style="display:none"><b>Transferance</b><br />Several years ago I was passing a charity shop and through the window, I spotted a number of books for sale, so I ventured inside. This book really stood out so I bought it. I was absolutely intrigued when I realised that the main protagonist (Ariel Manto) bought her copy of <i>The End of Mr Y</i> in a second-hand bookshop. I opened the cover and started reading hoping that my expectation of a fantasy adventure wasn’t just about to be smashed. <br /><br />From the first page, I was hooked, an immediate recognition that this was going to be highly original and I’d better be prepared for new perceptions and twists through Ariel’s pursuit of Thomas Lumas (the fictional author of the “End of Mr Y”). It is a very clever plot where the reader is taken on a journey through the Troposphere, a place where you can travel through time and other people’s thoughts. There was definitely a risk that this could just get too unbelievable and silly but I continued to remain connected to the characters and their complexity. The plot moved at a great pace and remained fascinating. The science wasn't a big issue for me but Scarlett Thomas does seem to put some weight behind it. I found it interesting but not compelling and if it’s not your thing, you can leave to the side without affecting the story.<br /><br />I bought all Scarlett Thomas’ books on the basis of this novel but none have had the same impact as this one. I have a fantasy connection to this book (even if fabricated in only my own mind). While I feel I have contributed significantly to Amazon’s success :) I will keep trying to find books in unexpected places hoping to be rewarded with another experience like this.<br /><br />I would recommend this book and would rate it 4.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6760505447727455528" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11459031486975212823">Holy #&*#*$!@ Christ, Scarlett Thomas has taken the top of my head off. I thought PopCo was an awesome mindf!ck, but Mr. Y makes it look like so much People magazine. <br/><br/>I'm really not sure what to say about this novel. I think people that like House of Leaves would probably like it for similar reasons though it's not nearly so hard to follow. Her female lead, as in PopCo, is almost frighteningly intelligent, as I'm beginning to suspect Thomas is herself. It's not the intelligence that's enticing a</span>
  <span id="freeText11459031486975212823" style="display:none">Holy #&amp;*#*$!@ Christ, Scarlett Thomas has taken the top of my head off. I thought PopCo was an awesome mindf!ck, but Mr. Y makes it look like so much People magazine. <br /><br />I'm really not sure what to say about this novel. I think people that like House of Leaves would probably like it for similar reasons though it's not nearly so hard to follow. Her female lead, as in PopCo, is almost frighteningly intelligent, as I'm beginning to suspect Thomas is herself. It's not the intelligence that's enticing and delightful, it's the limitless sense of curiosity and whimsy she brings to life. It may not be realistic to want to be a PhD student with no money, endless cigarettes and thousands of books and only the directive to solve life's tangled mysteries, but DAMN, it sure is alluring.<br /><br />In trying to describe the book today to a friend, I explained it, like, it's the thinkiest heart-in-your-mouth thriller you could imagine reading. There's Einstein and Heidigger by the boatload but hey, there's also a terrifying telepathic chase across Southern England! And freaky autistic ghosts that eat your brain! And lots of Derrida!<br /><br />If I had to compare her to anyone, it would be humbly to David Mitchell. Not that she's exactly reaching the penthouse apartment of genius that is Cloud Atlas, but damn if she's not at least in the same building. With a good view, even.</span>

<div id="review_68864178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68864178" />
    <a title="Batsap" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2655403-batsap"><img alt="Batsap" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362917893p2/2655403.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/68864178?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Batsap" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Batsap" href="/user/show/2655403-batsap">Batsap</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670700" href="/book/show/2153792.The_End_of_Mr_Y">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2655403-batsap?shelf=not-good">not-good</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2655403-batsap?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68864178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14103180247658968400">I couldn't wait to finish this book... but not because I was hooked, perched on the edge of my seat as I desperately waited to see how everything turned out, I just wanted the tedium to end.<br/><br/>It wasn't even the constant drip-feed of Philosophy and Quantum Physics that had me yawning and searching for the nearest caffiene source. In fact, that was the most interesting part of the book. (If you can overlook the obsessive name-dropping and reference to Derrida on almost <i>every single page</i>.) The way it</span>
  <span id="freeText14103180247658968400" style="display:none">I couldn't wait to finish this book... but not because I was hooked, perched on the edge of my seat as I desperately waited to see how everything turned out, I just wanted the tedium to end.<br /><br />It wasn't even the constant drip-feed of Philosophy and Quantum Physics that had me yawning and searching for the nearest caffiene source. In fact, that was the most interesting part of the book. (If you can overlook the obsessive name-dropping and reference to Derrida on almost <i>every single page</i>.) The way it was presented though - in pretentious conversations between equally pretentious characters - made it difficult to connect with what was actually being discussed.<br /><br />Which brings me to what I found most deadly about the book: the main character Ariel Manto. In my opinion she was the worst kind of female stereotype. In a desperate bid to perhaps show that she was 'in' with students, or even just to appear modern, Thomas had her protaganist blantantly smoking, swearing, sleeping around and whining about her terrible upbringing. And it wasn't just the charming Miss Manto, all the characters were shallow stereotypes trying too hard to be 'cool'. <br /><br />Usually, the plot or the style of writing can make up for a lack of interesting characters, but sadly this was not the case. Judging by the plot, this would have looked more at home in the children's section, if it weren't for the gratuitous sex and swearing - which I suppose were put in just to prove that the book was for adults. As for the writing style, it was fair enough with some good turns of phrase, but nowhere near good enough to make up for the tepid plot and annoying characters.<br /><br />So, if you have an interest in thought experiments and the nature of the universe, I suggest you pick up a Philosophy or popular Science book instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14103180247658968400" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15530185324582000517">This is perhaps the worst book I ever finished. I don't really recommend it. The thought experiment aspect of the book could have been interesting, but was unfortunately written for people who haven't read Baudrillard and don't understand particle physics.   <span id="freeText15530185324582000517" style="display:none">This is perhaps the worst book I ever finished. I don't really recommend it. The thought experiment aspect of the book could have been interesting, but was unfortunately written for people who haven't read Baudrillard and don't understand particle physics. Which I don't, but I got it much faster than the people in the book. Plus, the story was absurd, and poorly thought out. The main character was smarter than the writer, and seemed to resent that. Plus, it seemed that the sex scenes were written by someone who knows nothing about sex about someone who has serious sex issues. It was almost unbearable. I only finished the book because it was short and I have an obsession with finishing books. I wouldn't recommend anyone else try it. It doesn't get any better. But I gave it 2 stars because I finished it.</span>

<div id="review_98802472" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98802472" />
    <a title="Charles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3486245-charles"><img alt="Charles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578265360p2/3486245.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98802472?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles" href="/user/show/3486245-charles">Charles</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98802472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3915984147063975387">Note to authors: Merely mentioning "Husserl" or "Derrida" does not make a book intellectual or philosophical. Similarly, uttering the name -- much less quoting -- "Einstein," "Heisenberg," or "Schrodinger" does not make a book scientific, or lend credibility to the writing.<br/><br/>To those who have positively reviewed this book noting its intellectualism or creativity or surprise ending, I am glad that you enjoyed it.<br/><br/>I thought the book pointless, rambling, and pseduointellectual. The author quoted the n</span>
  <span id="freeText3915984147063975387" style="display:none">Note to authors: Merely mentioning "Husserl" or "Derrida" does not make a book intellectual or philosophical. Similarly, uttering the name -- much less quoting -- "Einstein," "Heisenberg," or "Schrodinger" does not make a book scientific, or lend credibility to the writing.<br /><br />To those who have positively reviewed this book noting its intellectualism or creativity or surprise ending, I am glad that you enjoyed it.<br /><br />I thought the book pointless, rambling, and pseduointellectual. The author quoted the names of giants of quantum physics, hoping to fool the poor reader that this was really physics, or scientifically plausible, when, in fact, the scientific application was simply obscure, opaque, or incorrect.<br /><br />Maybe I am tired of science fiction as a style, or casually referencing multiple dimensions, or time travel, or the concept of jumping into and out of other people's minds and memories as blithely or effortlessly as jumping into and out of a wading pool. Science fiction, is, ultimately, more fiction than science; and, really, that's OK. It doesn't have to be true, or provable; it just can't be silly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3915984147063975387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1739399007376830699">Like Thomas' <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4285468.PopCo' title='PopCo by Scarlett Thomas' rel='nofollow noopener'> PopCo</a>, I found this both fascinating and frustrating. Thomas definitely achieves something really special with her ability to make her writing intensely cerebral (some of my favorite parts of <i>Mr. Y</i> were the digressions into quantum physics and other brain-stretching topics) while at the same time creating very human, flawed characters. Still, there's a quality of...coldness that prevents me from becoming emotionally involved. Perhaps the whole thing seems too clever, too orchestrat</span>
  <span id="freeText1739399007376830699" style="display:none">Like Thomas' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4285468.PopCo" title="PopCo by Scarlett Thomas" rel="nofollow noopener"> PopCo</a>, I found this both fascinating and frustrating. Thomas definitely achieves something really special with her ability to make her writing intensely cerebral (some of my favorite parts of <i>Mr. Y</i> were the digressions into quantum physics and other brain-stretching topics) while at the same time creating very human, flawed characters. Still, there's a quality of...coldness that prevents me from becoming emotionally involved. Perhaps the whole thing seems too clever, too orchestrated? I don't know. Anyway: the plot of this novel is nominally about a cursed book, but is really much more like an alternate take on <i>Being John Malkovich</i> with an ending that feels like the close of <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i>, the part that's supposed to be best if watched stoned. As with <i>PopCo</i>, the experience of reading the novel was very pleasurable and interesting, but the final impact just isn't there; it's oddly unsatisfying.</span>

<div id="review_36480264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36480264" />
    <a title="Aaron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/323130-aaron"><img alt="Aaron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1188492019p2/323130.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36480264?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron" href="/user/show/323130-aaron">Aaron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer36480264&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36480264" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7674652654068559816">Imagine a novel that has never been read by anyone who has lived to talk about it. The only people known to have finished it either died shortly afterward or have disappeared without a trace. The novel in question is so notorious that the only copy known to exist has been locked in a vault in Berlin, preventing anyone from ever reading it again. This novel, for all practical purposes, is cursed. If you read it, you will die.<br/><br/>If such a novel existed and you found a copy in a used bookstore, would </span>
  <span id="freeText7674652654068559816" style="display:none">Imagine a novel that has never been read by anyone who has lived to talk about it. The only people known to have finished it either died shortly afterward or have disappeared without a trace. The novel in question is so notorious that the only copy known to exist has been locked in a vault in Berlin, preventing anyone from ever reading it again. This novel, for all practical purposes, is cursed. If you read it, you will die.<br /><br />If such a novel existed and you found a copy in a used bookstore, would you read it?<br /><br />Ariel Manto would. In fact, she does.<br /><br />This idea, the notion of reading a novel known to be cursed is the premise at the heart of Scarlett Thomas's well-written, thought-provoking, but ultimately disappointing new novel.<br /><br /><br />The novel within the novel contains a recipe for a tincture that if you mix, drink, and then stare into a hand-drawn black dot allows you to exit this dimension into another (referred to in the novel as The Troposphere). In this dimension, you have access (through a process called paradesis) to the minds of any sentient being who has ever been in any way related to anyone you have ever known. This includes animals as well, especially mice or felines. Anyone who enters this dimension has their minds opened, to heretofore unknown truths about life. Truths about God. Truths about scientific law. Truths about existence itself.<br /><br />This is a thought-provoking novel full of well-written, well-thought-out, well-organized postulations about reality, philosophy, and religion. One of my favorites is a discussion about whether Einstein's famous energy formula is actually something he proved to be true or something he willed to be true, forcing humanity to accept its veracity.<br /><br />As it turns out, it isn't the novel itself that will kill you, but the people who want the recipe contained therein and will stop at nothing to get it. If you read the novel and enter The Troposphere, they can find you and they will more than likely catch you.<br /><br />I have many problems with this novel, despite how thought-provoking it is, the least of which being the protagonist. She is described as a sexual deviant, a self mutilator, and one waits for this to have some bearing on the story being held. But it doesn't. It just is. Shocking material for the sake of shocking material has never really had much appeal for me. The bigger issue is the love story that permeates this novel. It also has no true bearing on the story (other than to allow Romeo to show up at the last minute and fix everything with both guns blazing) and kind of bogs down the philosophical discussion. The discussion is key to what makes this novel so interesting to read, but perhaps too many idea are set forth. The last forty pages go by too quickly with nothing really getting solved. And the ending is abrupt. <br /><br />In the end, I really only care about the philosophical questions I was left to think about. The novel itself will fade from my memory.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7674652654068559816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_30336763" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30336763" />
    <a title="Amber" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1432865-amber"><img alt="Amber" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228874539p2/1432865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30336763?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amber" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amber" href="/user/show/1432865-amber">Amber</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670700" href="/book/show/2153792.The_End_of_Mr_Y">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">posers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432865-amber?shelf=read-adult">read-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432865-amber?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432865-amber?shelf=abandoning">abandoning</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30336763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8659235255650300391">What a bloody waste of a good idea! First off, the concept behind this book is brilliant. An eighteenth century writer and metaphysicist writes a book which contains within it an alchemy-like recipe which will allow the reader to enter the realm of disembodied thought. Cool! And it's full of philosophy and bizarre adventures. Double cool!<br/><br/>AND THE AUTHOR (and her protagonist) ARE COMPLETELY UP THEIR OWN ARSES. FAIL!!!!<br/><br/>Now, maybe you can get past vomit-inducingly bad sentences like this:<br/>"Sometimes,</span>
  <span id="freeText8659235255650300391" style="display:none">What a bloody waste of a good idea! First off, the concept behind this book is brilliant. An eighteenth century writer and metaphysicist writes a book which contains within it an alchemy-like recipe which will allow the reader to enter the realm of disembodied thought. Cool! And it's full of philosophy and bizarre adventures. Double cool!<br /><br />AND THE AUTHOR (and her protagonist) ARE COMPLETELY UP THEIR OWN ARSES. FAIL!!!!<br /><br />Now, maybe you can get past vomit-inducingly bad sentences like this:<br />"Sometimes, on these January afternoons, the sun squats low in the sky like an orange-robed Buddha in a documenary on the meaning of life."<br /><br />Which is good, because they're everywhere. But can you get past an author who spends an entire novel trying to convince you that she's "the smartest one in the room"? Ms Thomas is so impressed by her own wit and intelligence, so thoughtless of her reader, that she sees fit to name-drop Derrida and "differance" and Flatland and whatever else in nearly every chapter, not in a way that draws the curious reader into these concepts but in a way designed -- like those people who name-drop in conversations without ever bothering to ask their listeners if they know what the heck they're talking about -- to exclude nearly everyone.<br /><br />Now, I've read Derrida and Flatland. I've read "Simulacra and Simulation" and "The Outsider" and Husserl and Heidegger. Maybe this is why I found all of her constant referencing so annoying. Because **she doesn't know what the heck she's talking about**. Her plotline about the mental videogame and mousesex or whatever has very little to do with the idea of a simulacrum. The name-dropping is an excuse for lack of real thought -- it's exactly what undergrad students of philosophy do in order to gain purchase and lend credibility to themselves when really, what they have to say is really not all that deep or interesting at all. Because the name dropping is enough to snap all of the lesser posers into place, because it's not meant to excite someone about new ideas, it's meant to exclude (and make the excluded clambor puppy-like towards inclusion, meaning be damned!).<br /><br />Yes, you can say differance is about no reference ever being completely shared and meaning being infinitely delayed and blah-de-blah -- but the fact remains that Derrida's work is based on a well-considered conceptual framework (whether you buy it or not), and this is pretty shoddily thought-out work -- underneath all of the critical theory hoo-ha and esoteric subculture claptrap. Someone should tell Ms Thomas that associating oneself with great minds does not make one's own mind great. I'd like to see anyone with any real understanding of philosophy read pages 283 - 287 and not laugh and laugh.<br /><br />There are also long chunks of text, which have nothing to do with the story really, where Ms. Thomas waffles on about random pseudo-philosophical topics, the way a high school student does after they've seen The Matrix for the first time. I mean, this woman really thinks she's smarter than nearly all of her readers, and is convinced she can make you think that too. And as much as I like learning interesting facts (only elephants understand about death? That's pretty cool.) -- interesting facts do not a believable and engrossing fictional universe make. And the only thing worse than Ms Thomas is her main character, Ariel Manto, who is, in a word -- repulsive. She is unemotional, unlikeable, unbelievable --- and her author seems to be on a mission to convince us that she is "cool", by expounding on her troubled rebellious childhood and iPod art radio-listening habits.<br /><br />I'm not even going to get into the cold, empty, obsessive, and rather disgusting treatment of sex.<br /><br />So again, what a waste. I prefer smart books that are designed to bring their readers into the fold, not books that put on a show of being smart to try and dazzle you into thinking they have something to say. The End of Mr. Y has nothing interesting to say. It is a sham and -- I challenge you to dispute this after reading the idiotic ending -- a complete waste of time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8659235255650300391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review30336763">

<div id="review_665674323" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/665674323" />
    <a title="Gregsamsa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22119193-gregsamsa"><img alt="Gregsamsa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374800548p2/22119193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/665674323?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gregsamsa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gregsamsa" href="/user/show/22119193-gregsamsa">Gregsamsa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer665674323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11111506597461094342">I have Scarlett Thomas to thank for a little embarrassing moment of discovering another little nugget of my own sexism. I thought, <i>I can't dis- this book and give it one star.</i> But why not? Because she's a she and I don't want to be mean? Better to be honest: this book is, I'm just going to say it: STUPID.<br/><br/>The hype totally had me; I couldn't <i>not</i> read it: thought experiments (none actually handled), the nature of consciousness (adding an alternate reality does not interrogate the subject), Derrida </span>
  <span id="freeText11111506597461094342" style="display:none">I have Scarlett Thomas to thank for a little embarrassing moment of discovering another little nugget of my own sexism. I thought, <i>I can't dis- this book and give it one star.</i> But why not? Because she's a she and I don't want to be mean? Better to be honest: this book is, I'm just going to say it: STUPID.<br /><br />The hype totally had me; I couldn't <i>not</i> read it: thought experiments (none actually handled), the nature of consciousness (adding an alternate reality does not interrogate the subject), Derrida (namedropped only, save for a few reductive squeaks, like "meaning is fluid" oh, is it?), causal chains and temporality (real problems of "what if" variety summarily dismissed), quantum physics (nope, not a bit). I don't guess it's dishonest to say that the book <i>contains</i> these things, since they are mentioned, but if you're looking for an actual exploration of these subjects or even a competent handling of them within a narrative rather than an extremely dorky soft sci-fi story overburdened with clunky expositional dialog and cute ironies, you will be disappointed. The End of Mr.Y. The End of Mister Y. The End of <i>Mystery</i>. Get it? The book is never more clever than <i>that</i>.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11111506597461094342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_782272203" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/782272203" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/782272203?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670700" href="/book/show/2153792.The_End_of_Mr_Y">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=sff">sff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-2000s">decade-2000s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=orange-prize">orange-prize</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer782272203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17076354669578131721">Whilst I agree that this has been oversold as far as unswervingly highbrow or hard SF readers are concerned, it's got plenty going for it as a character-driven fantasy novel, and Scarlett Thomas is becoming my new favourite comfort-reading author. (Even within my friends list, opinions of <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> range from "highly intellectual yet accessible" to "completely stupid", so this isn't the easiest review I've ever written.)<br/><br/>The book is, admittedly, comfortable because of its blend of familiar</span>
  <span id="freeText17076354669578131721" style="display:none">Whilst I agree that this has been oversold as far as unswervingly highbrow or hard SF readers are concerned, it's got plenty going for it as a character-driven fantasy novel, and Scarlett Thomas is becoming my new favourite comfort-reading author. (Even within my friends list, opinions of <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> range from "highly intellectual yet accessible" to "completely stupid", so this isn't the easiest review I've ever written.)<br /><br />The book is, admittedly, comfortable because of its blend of familiar things I already love: impoverished postgrad Ariel has adventures whilst investigating work of fictional Victorian author (<i>Possession</i>, though the C19th pastiche extracts aren't half as good), drinks magic potion like Alice, encounters concepts from Pratchett's <i>Small Gods</i> and enters other minds like Granny Weatherwax gone borrowing or all those body-swap movies from the late 80s, and there's some <i>Matrix</i> type stuff for good measure. The overall tone is reminiscent of some of the stories in Neil Gaiman's wonderful collection <i>Smoke and Mirrors</i>, the narrative and plot being pushed by a protagonist with a strong personality and a darkish past and present, rather than by the SFF content itself. There's an argument for Scarlett Thomas's work as weirder, darker, more academically-inclined chicklit, but on the other hand, Ariel's angst and lust, and Thomas' moments of acute insight into messed-up people (esp. p277ish; also 345, 411 and all over the place really) are hardly different from the portrayals in Gaiman's male character-focused stories. (Unlike chicklit characters, Ariel never goes "huh! men!", tacitly seeming not to find them particularly 'other', and she hardly ever makes whole-gender-based generalisations. She spends a helluva lot of time thinking about other things [interesting things, not handbags and bad chardonnay] as well as love and sex, without seeing that as an either/or ... Thomas keeps writing characters I want to be friends with.) And as in <i>Smoke and Mirrors</i>, there are a couple of scenes sexier than plenty of stuff marketed as erotica - though there isn't as much shagging as some of the blurb quotes imply.<br /><br />Reading posts about entirely different books made me realise that <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> will be of interest to people who like reversal of traditional gender roles in fantasy stories. Ariel is driving the adventure throughout, and her male love/lust interests are, as the adventure gets going, as peripheral and helpmeetish as they would be for a man in a typical action movie. This is perhaps less the case towards the end but I might just see it that way because I'm a bitter old hag <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[who doesn't like characters experiencing unrealistically fulfilling romance, despite sometimes having falling asleep to ridiculous merging dreams like the final chapters. Still, it shows plenty of insight into the characters' psychology as it's an impossible wish-fulfilment concomitant with their troubles - to which the realist contrast is what Burlem says on p.411 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. At the same time I could still think of plenty of stories in which the they would have been the other way round.<br /><br />There's ample comment on Goodreads about inaccuracies in the characters' academic discussions (especially re. physics and the "linguistic turn" of philosophy and literary theory. The people who are complaining that homeopathy has an effect in the book simply need a reminder that this is <i>fantasy fiction</i> and that the works of Ben Goldacre can be overapplied.) The media has presented <i>Mr. Y</i> as formidably clever - to the chagrin of people who picked it up expecting some novelised <i>Godel Escher Bach</i> - but the tone of everything I read between the covers is casual musings from bright non-specialists ... it sounds like (and in some cases in the book <i>is</i>) conversations with friends after which you might realise you got something slightly wrong. If, for instance, Eng lit, theology and biology academics are riffing semi-drunkenly about what they've read in popular science books on quantum physics, what's here sounds fine in that context. (If I was joining in the pickiness, I'd say that Lamarck was presented as entirely infra-dig, although epigenetics - a topic that had already found its way into BBC documentaries a couple of years before <i>Mr. Y</i> was published - has given a new slant on his ideas. But then I realised that wasn't necessarily the point or the spirit of the book.) However I would have liked to have seen a little more determined accuracy from the narrator in tone as well as content in some of her home subject areas - if you're a postgrad you need to do that alongside the flights of enthusiasm.<br /><br />But in the end the academic conversations are icing; you don't really need them to enjoy the rest of the book if you don't like them. The fantasy story is fine regardless - the chat and speculation is just how the characters look at their experience. Which I find more interesting than an unreflective adventure, though they do go on a bit sometimes. <br /><br />I may only have given it four stars due to its faults and moments of derivativeness but I really loved <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> as I love too few books as an end in themselves. (Often I don't understand what the rest of you are on about re. loving books generally... A couple of years ago, a remover said, seeing the book cases, "So you like reading then?" The first, silent, response that came into my head, "Not really, but what the bloody hell else am I supposed to do?" ...Aloud, I made a vaguely affirmative non-verbal noise and started talking about cardboard boxes.) I was glad it was there when I couldn't sleep, I span it out so I could keep going back into its world - its plot analogy for addiction to an imaginary world actually applied - rather than trying to get it finished and ticked off. I'd have been delighted were it 1000 pages not 500, or if it was part of a series as long as Discworld. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17076354669578131721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_114029192" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114029192" />
    <a title="Lauren Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2109160-lauren-smith"><img alt="Lauren Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515755113p2/2109160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114029192?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren Smith" href="/user/show/2109160-lauren-smith">Lauren Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670700" href="/book/show/2153792.The_End_of_Mr_Y">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=to-re-read">to-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=language">language</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=postmodern">postmodern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=metafiction">metafiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109160-lauren-smith?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114029192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9438768472462161870">This is an adventure in thought experiments. This is idea porn. It's the most cerebral fun I’ve ever had. The End of Mr Y is a cocktail of postmodern philosophy, quantum physics, metafiction, science fiction and adventure. If any of that sounds intimidating, rest assured that this isn’t like reading Derrida, Heidegger, Baudrillard or any of the convoluted philosophies that Ariel Manto likes to immerse herself in. Early on she says that she “quite like[s] the way you can talk about science withou</span>
  <span id="freeText9438768472462161870" style="display:none">This is an adventure in thought experiments. This is idea porn. It's the most cerebral fun I’ve ever had. The End of Mr Y is a cocktail of postmodern philosophy, quantum physics, metafiction, science fiction and adventure. If any of that sounds intimidating, rest assured that this isn’t like reading Derrida, Heidegger, Baudrillard or any of the convoluted philosophies that Ariel Manto likes to immerse herself in. Early on she says that she “quite like[s] the way you can talk about science without necessarily using mathematics, but using metaphors instead” (29) and that really goes for all the key theories so beautifully woven into the story. <i>The End of Mr Y</i> is in itself a thought experiment for all the science and philosophy it explores, a mind-warping vision of a postmodern existence where language creates reality rather than just describing it.<br /><br />Read the full review on my blog <a href="http://violininavoid.wordpress.com/2010/09/29/review-the-end-of-mr-y/" rel="nofollow noopener">Violin in a Void</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9438768472462161870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1280055603" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1280055603" />
    <a title="F.R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1309606-f-r"><img alt="F.R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544886612p2/1309606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1280055603?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="F.R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="F.R." href="/user/show/1309606-f-r">F.R.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670700" href="/book/show/2153792.The_End_of_Mr_Y">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1280055603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14889810385187268590">There are some books you read where you know incredibly swiftly that you absolutely LOVE them. You get about a quarter of the way through and find yourself totally entranced by just how much you’re enjoying every single page, but it’s an entrancement mixed with a kind of niggling dread where you whisper to the book: “Please don’t let me down! Please don’t let me down!” You sit there reading and desperately praying: “Please don’t be this good and then tumble away to crap! Don’t do an Icarus and c</span>
  <span id="freeText14889810385187268590" style="display:none">There are some books you read where you know incredibly swiftly that you absolutely LOVE them. You get about a quarter of the way through and find yourself totally entranced by just how much you’re enjoying every single page, but it’s an entrancement mixed with a kind of niggling dread where you whisper to the book: “Please don’t let me down! Please don’t let me down!” You sit there reading and desperately praying: “Please don’t be this good and then tumble away to crap! Don’t do an Icarus and crash to Earth, your second half being a pale shadow of your brilliant early self. Please don’t! Please don’t!”<br /><br />That was my experience on reading ‘The End of Mr Y’.<br /><br />And fortunately it didn’t down.<br /> <br />There are some books that you just connect with. You get them and they get you. I am genetically pre-programmed to enjoy reading about a young, mixed up, red-head academic and her quest for meaning in life; but more than that, the unashamed bibliophile part of me was always going to be excited when that quest led to her tracking down the last copy of a seemingly cursed book. (In addition I particularly enjoyed that that cursed book seemed to fit in seamlessly to a curious minded part of Victoriana.) Then I liked that there was so much popular science and philosophy dropped in. Now, to be fair, I don’t read much of that stuff myself but I always like to learn by osmosis. And it goes almost without saying that I was always going to love that this is a book packed with wonderful sentences like: <i>“Monday morning, and the sky is the colour of sad weddings.”</i> While even when this novel swerves into fantasy, which at other points in my life I’d have found a turn off, it hit me in the perfect place, my mind receptive to every turn. In short this is a book which felt made specifically for me. Okay, even though I loved it, I can’t imagine that it’s going to be a perfect match for everyone, it certainly won’t fit like a literary glove to everybody’s tastes – but if you are on the right wavelength, this is a truly beautiful, fantastical, intelligent and amazing piece of work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14889810385187268590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_617981360" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/617981360" />
    <a title="Tim Hicks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111076-tim-hicks"><img alt="Tim Hicks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327258501p2/111076.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/617981360?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim Hicks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim Hicks" href="/user/show/111076-tim-hicks">Tim Hicks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer617981360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15144001192940421425">What a load of old cobblers that was. <br/><br/>I recognized right away the standard British plot with a not-very-successful plodder at a university where things aren't going well and she has no money and she just keeps on with the I-shouldn't-be-doing-this habits, and OF COURSE she spends the last of her money on a book. Someone from about 80 years ago ought to get royalties whenever this tired old plot framework is used. <br/> <br/>Thomas has made a bit of an effort to modernize the book. Our heroine has an iPod,</span>
  <span id="freeText15144001192940421425" style="display:none">What a load of old cobblers that was. <br /><br />I recognized right away the standard British plot with a not-very-successful plodder at a university where things aren't going well and she has no money and she just keeps on with the I-shouldn't-be-doing-this habits, and OF COURSE she spends the last of her money on a book. Someone from about 80 years ago ought to get royalties whenever this tired old plot framework is used. <br /> <br />Thomas has made a bit of an effort to modernize the book. Our heroine has an iPod, wow, and isn't opposed to using sex to get what she needs. She's experimented with cutting and pain sex. Sorry, we left that behind with Girl with the Dragon Tattoo. <br /> <br />Then we get the Derrida and Heidegger, and right away I thought, "Oh, no, here we go, a long boring plot that's going to end by disappearing up its own nether orifice." I've never been so unpleased to be right. <br /><br />Then the homeopathy. Always a good basis for moving a "science fiction" story ahead. Yeah. Makes alchemy and magic look so complicated and wasteful. Even if they are MORE LOGICAL AND RIGOROUS -and indeed, just as effective. <br /><br />And don't even get me started on the epilogue. GAAAAAHHH! <br /><br />If you thought The Matrix stuck too closely to logic and reason, you'l love this book. <br /><br />Soon I'm off to the library to see if I can persuade them to reclassify this steaming pile of that-which-is-good-for-the-garden to New Age Philosophy where it belongs.<br /><br />Or maybe I'll mix some water with water and see if I can go into a space where I can jump through time and space via mice until I can persuade the author not to write this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15144001192940421425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1526138979" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1526138979" />

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31120889-nivedita?shelf=2016">2016</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1526138979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6927391540240920442">When you pick up a book with an intriguing cover and an even more intriguing synopsis, you buy it. Then you look it up on Goodreads, and it's full of mixed reviews, but yet you give it a try. And then when it fails to deliver you anything that you expected it to give, you just stop reading it and put it away; never too see it ever. <br/>When I started reading it, I was very excited. But soon, I knew I was wrong. Everything was absurd. The characters, the writing, the characters of the book inside thi</span>
  <span id="freeText6927391540240920442" style="display:none">When you pick up a book with an intriguing cover and an even more intriguing synopsis, you buy it. Then you look it up on Goodreads, and it's full of mixed reviews, but yet you give it a try. And then when it fails to deliver you anything that you expected it to give, you just stop reading it and put it away; never too see it ever. <br />When I started reading it, I was very excited. But soon, I knew I was wrong. Everything was absurd. The characters, the writing, the characters of the book inside this book, the writing of the books inside this book. Everything was going completely wrong. This should have been my sign to stop it. To not continue. But still I continued. <br />To say I was bored is not even close to an expression to convey how I felt when concepts of quantum physics or actually science, in general, was randomly dropped everywhere in the book. The main protagonist was a deadly mix of stupidity, desperation and even like stupidity.<br />To make the matters even worse, the author introduces even more random characters who are as pretentious as the main character. <br />All this just happened within 50% of the book.<br />I'm sorry but I cannot continue to find out what happens in the next 50% of the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6927391540240920442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1844631551155492772">I tried to write paragraphs about what precisely I hated on this book and that just made me even more annoyed than the reading experience was. But some details: special relativity and homeopathy both "work" and got equal footing and are treated the same way by our main character, which btw is a lit PhD student who needs several days full time to read a book of about 150 pages. No, she is not taking notes, she does that later. Ah, and I can´t figure out when the book is supposed to be set: it was</span>
  <span id="freeText1844631551155492772" style="display:none">I tried to write paragraphs about what precisely I hated on this book and that just made me even more annoyed than the reading experience was. But some details: special relativity and homeopathy both "work" and got equal footing and are treated the same way by our main character, which btw is a lit PhD student who needs several days full time to read a book of about 150 pages. No, she is not taking notes, she does that later. Ah, and I can´t figure out when the book is supposed to be set: it was published in 2007, and there are mentions to blogs and ipods which put me off because otherwise the little IT details made me think it was set in the 90s. I miss 404 messages - or well, no, not really, but I am getting nostalgic about how the net was 10 or 15 years ago.<br /><br />About the serious things, main character is not interesting, secondary characters seem to be there to give more excuses for lots of postmodernist philosophizing, and I quit reading it seriously halfway and skimmed the rest to see if it improved. Got worse if anything. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25202229?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847670700" href="/book/show/2153792.The_End_of_Mr_Y">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=sff">sff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=2000s-release">2000s-release</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25202229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10846452363974596886">I really, really loved roughly the first two-thirds of this book, and it might have been a five-star read had the final portion (and the ending in particular) not turned out to be such a disappointment. Admittedly, I've never been a huge fan of fiction that strays too far into fantasy, and I felt <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> became so detached from any recognisable world that I stopped caring what happened. The further Ariel ventured into the Troposphere, the more ridiculous the story became - the last few </span>
  <span id="freeText10846452363974596886" style="display:none">I really, really loved roughly the first two-thirds of this book, and it might have been a five-star read had the final portion (and the ending in particular) not turned out to be such a disappointment. Admittedly, I've never been a huge fan of fiction that strays too far into fantasy, and I felt <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> became so detached from any recognisable world that I stopped caring what happened. The further Ariel ventured into the Troposphere, the more ridiculous the story became - the last few chapters almost felt as though the author was bored of writing the novel and couldn't wait to get it finished. It's a shame since I loved the rest of the story - the book is intelligent but very accessible, probably my favourite type of writing. I'd still recommend it to everyone, but I just wish the conclusion could have been more comprehensible and grounded in reality.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10846452363974596886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_88293569" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/88293569" />
    <a title="Annelien" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/755942-annelien"><img alt="Annelien" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1467926386p2/755942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/88293569?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annelien" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annelien" href="/user/show/755942-annelien">Annelien</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">philosophers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Annelien by: </span>
      /
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/755942-annelien?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/755942-annelien?shelf=detective">detective</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/755942-annelien?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88293569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5399198086994988241">This is a good example of good idea executed badly. I liked the concept and the first 25 pages. Ariel Manto is doing a Phd on a strange author from the 1800s and by accident comes across his rarest book in a used bookstore. What follows is an account of what she does with the contents of the book and the suspense of a mystery involving two creepy American policemen who are out to get her, it seems.<br/><br/>but in reality, what follows is the author showcasing how much she knows about philosophy and how m</span>
  <span id="freeText5399198086994988241" style="display:none">This is a good example of good idea executed badly. I liked the concept and the first 25 pages. Ariel Manto is doing a Phd on a strange author from the 1800s and by accident comes across his rarest book in a used bookstore. What follows is an account of what she does with the contents of the book and the suspense of a mystery involving two creepy American policemen who are out to get her, it seems.<br /><br />but in reality, what follows is the author showcasing how much she knows about philosophy and how much of it she can sneak into the plotline. Don't get me wrong, i love a well-researched novel and it's very nice to have a little background to the world she creates, but too much is too much. I can't bear anymore Heidegger or Derida. I didn't really learn much about philosophy either. Just that it's very complicated.<br /><br />I think that this is a very nice read if you know lots and lots about philosophy and are interested in seeing their theories applied in a book.<br /><br />There were some ideas in there that I liked (the trains, the troposphere) but they weren't really explained thoroughly. The plot, when stripped of all the fancyness, seems a little meagre. the characters weren't very appealing. but, still, a good idea. <br /><br />book: 10/50<br />pages: 3350</span>
  <a data-text-id="5399198086994988241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3023651895" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3023651895" />
    <a title="Anne Vivliohomme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57605400-anne-vivliohomme"><img alt="Anne Vivliohomme" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526140680p2/57605400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3023651895?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne Vivliohomme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne Vivliohomme" href="/user/show/57605400-anne-vivliohomme">Anne Vivliohomme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9781847672292" href="/book/show/3237247-the-end-of-mr-y">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57605400-anne-vivliohomme?shelf=something-borrowed">something-borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3023651895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9656205736213160862">Someone needs to catch up on evolution, because boy, so many things in this book confused me. First of all, consciousness evolved from trees? How the hell did animals get consciousness then? Animals didn't evolve from trees... Can someone please explain to me how consciousness started with trees (and why don't trees have a conscious as of today?)<br/><br/>This book is one of the most mind-boggling books I have ever read. I loved how so many scientific theories made an appearance in this book. Actually, th</span>
  <span id="freeText9656205736213160862" style="display:none">Someone needs to catch up on evolution, because boy, so many things in this book confused me. First of all, consciousness evolved from trees? How the hell did animals get consciousness then? Animals didn't evolve from trees... Can someone please explain to me how consciousness started with trees (and why don't trees have a conscious as of today?)<br /><br />This book is one of the most mind-boggling books I have ever read. I loved how so many scientific theories made an appearance in this book. Actually, that's wrong. Scientific theories didn't only make an appearance, they <i>were</i> this book. I absolutely loved that about it. <i>The End of Mr. Y</i> is a mind experiment of its own, and I adored how all the theories made me question the fundaments of existence.<br /><br />However, novels can't exist with only scientific theories (unless you're Bill Bryson) and they need characters. That's where I started disliking the book. The characters. The author forces Ariel's specialness on the reader, and still I didn't care a mouse's ass about her. Halfway through the book I wished for her to be stuck into a coma so the book finally ended. Ariel has "done bad things", she's damaged, she can't be with the person she loves because she is "bad for him", and at one point she literally says "I didn't think there was anything special about me. Now it turns out there is.". Well, congrats Ariel! You're oficially a special snowflake! And it's not just Ariel. All the other characters (apart from the God of Mice, who I loved for his absurdity) were shitty and more two-dimensional than an actual book. There's the live interest whose only quality is his sexy hair, there's the family of Ariel who was pure evil and gave her suuuuper traumatic past, there's the sex obsessed white man in a divorce, there's the professor who likes books (woohoo, what a surprise) and there are so many more characters who conveniently appeared when the next step in the story was needed. <br /><br />This is another issue I had with the book: everything happened just a tad bit too conveniently. She's searching for a book, and wow! it just happens to be in the first second hand bookstore she comes across! The suspense and build-up that intrigued me so much at the beginning of the book led to predictable events. The only thing that surprised me was how repetitive the scientific theories became. <br /><br />Honestly, I just wished the end of Ariel would be sooner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9656205736213160862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13337802894728605955">Allright... Where do I even begin?????.... <br/> This is probably the best book I can think of to describe a mind &*@!... really. The premise is genius... the scientific information is incredibly insightful... (Was she a former scientist?)... the complexity was astonishing. <br/> This is quite an understatement, as the multitude of layers in the story is perplexing... but, it is basically about the main character, Ariel's adventure in a fourth dimension called the "Troposphere" in which she can enter othe</span>
  <span id="freeText13337802894728605955" style="display:none">Allright... Where do I even begin?????.... <br /> This is probably the best book I can think of to describe a mind &amp;*@!... really. The premise is genius... the scientific information is incredibly insightful... (Was she a former scientist?)... the complexity was astonishing. <br /> This is quite an understatement, as the multitude of layers in the story is perplexing... but, it is basically about the main character, Ariel's adventure in a fourth dimension called the "Troposphere" in which she can enter others' heads &amp; theoretically change their thinking, actions, etc. She enters their heads through "doorways" that appear before her... the "Troposphere" is complete with a "console" (Think Scott Pilgrim-esque), exploration of morality, life, death, religion (The DaVinci Code, but done the correct way), Genius, well-thought layers of thinking, encompassing the humanities, the social sciences, &amp; the hard sciences (References include Darwin, Einstein, Socrates, etc., as well as suspense &amp; adventure that really makes you think &amp; question long established theories in your life. <br /> I believe the best way to describe this amazing book is "Inception" with "Being John Malcovich", including a dash of "The Matrix"... Quantum Physics, Deconstructionist Theory, Science Fiction, Philosophy, Psychological intrigue.... the recipe for an amazing adventure... &amp; one of the only books I can say with confidence truly transported me to another place, where I literally lost track of time. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9645146168760198737">DNF 20%<br/>I can’t read any more of this. Just no. NOPE. The concept at the start was interesting and I was intrigued. But it’s safe to say things started to fall apart for me on page 89 (the start of chapter 7). And it got worse from there. <br/>I just don’t want to waste my time with it. <br/>I come from a physics back ground so the theoretical quantum mechanics and particle physics intrigued me. I also love a good sci-fi, fantasy or something that makes you think. <br/>This just... nope. <br/>I even enjoy books that</span>
  <span id="freeText9645146168760198737" style="display:none">DNF 20%<br />I can’t read any more of this. Just no. NOPE. The concept at the start was interesting and I was intrigued. But it’s safe to say things started to fall apart for me on page 89 (the start of chapter 7). And it got worse from there. <br />I just don’t want to waste my time with it. <br />I come from a physics back ground so the theoretical quantum mechanics and particle physics intrigued me. I also love a good sci-fi, fantasy or something that makes you think. <br />This just... nope. <br />I even enjoy books that have a book within it and even on this part it failed. <br />I have skipped through to get a brief idea of the plot and I’m good. I don’t want to read anymore of this.<br />Whilst I’m sure this is for some people, I am certainly not one of them. <br />Not the worst book i’ve ever read... but certainly the worst i’ve read this year. <br />Let me treat you to some quotes that pretty much made me stop:<br />‘The sky is the colour of sad weddings.’ <br />And this....<br />‘I want to be penetrated by the shrapnel of a million explosions.’ That, as Fat-Amy, would say? Is NOT a good enough reason to use the word penetrate. <br />I read this for a book club and I suspect our next meet up will Be interesting. </span>
